The Orthodox View on the Major Sinner and Refutation of the Murji'a
Nasafi presents the Ahl al-Haqq position on the major sinner and distinguishes it from the Murji'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Haqq position on the major sinner, including the role of God's will and the refutation of the extreme Murji'a claim.

Core Idea
Faith is not nullified by major sins; ultimate judgment belongs to God's will.

In this passage, Nasafi clarifies the Sunni doctrine regarding a Muslim who commits a major sin without considering it lawful. He distinguishes the orthodox view from the extreme Murji'a who wrongly claim that sin does not harm faith. The lesson will explore the definition, the ruling, the deferral (irja) to God's will, and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how Nasafi defines the major sinner and what conditions are mentioned (not considering it lawful, not treating it lightly, etc.).
The definition is crucial for understanding the ruling that follows.
2
Understand the ruling and God's will
Focus on the part describing the fate of the major sinner who dies without repentance. Notice the emphasis on God's will (mashī'a) and the eventual outcome of Paradise.
This is a key difference from the Murji'a who guarantee Paradise.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa and the label 'Murji'a'
Read how Abu Hanifa was called Murji' and his response. Understand that 'irja' means deferment, and that he learned it from the angels.
This shows the orthodox use of irja is different from the extreme Murji'a.
4
Refute the extreme Murji'a claim
Consider the refutation: the extreme Murji'a say no Muslim is punished for major sins, like a bad deed does not harm faith. Nasafi rejects this by attributing it to Muqatil ibn Sulayman.
This clarifies that the orthodox view holds sin can bring punishment but not eternal hell.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق: إن من اقترف كبيرة غير مستحل لها ولا مستخف بها لغلبة شهوة أو حمية يرجو الله تعالى أن يغفر له ويخاف أن يعذبه فهذا اسمه مؤمن، بقي على ما كان عليه من الإيمان لم يزل عنه إيمانه ولم ينتقض، ولا يخرج أحد الإيمان إلا من الباب الذي دخل فيه، فحكمه أنه لو مات من غير توبة فلله فيه المشيئة إن شاء عفا عنه بفضله وكرمه أو يتركه وما معه من الإيمان والحسنات أو بشفاعة بعض الأخيار، وإن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة ولا يخلد في النار، وكان أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يسمى مرجئًا لتأخيره أمر صاحب الكبيرة إلى مشيئة الله تعالى، والإرجاء هو التأخير، وروى عنه أنه قيل له ممن أخذت الإرجاء، فقال: من الملائكة عليهم السلام حيث قالوا: لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا وزعمت المرجئة الخبيثة أن أحدًا من المسلمين لا يعاقب على شيء من الكبائر، وكما أن الحسنة لا تنفع مع الكفر فالسيئة لا تضر مع الإيمان حكى هذا القول عن مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير.
The people of truth said: Indeed, whoever commits a major sin without considering it lawful nor treating it lightly due to overwhelming desire or zeal, hoping that God will forgive him and fearing that He will punish him, then his name is a believer; he remains upon what he was of faith, his faith has not left him nor has it been invalidated, and one does not exit from faith except through the door by which he entered. His ruling is that if he dies without repentance, then God has the will concerning him: if He wills, He forgives him by His bounty and generosity, or He leaves him with what he has of faith and good deeds or through the intercession of some righteous ones; and if He wills, He punishes him according to his sin, then his final outcome is Paradise inevitably, and he will not abide eternally in the Fire. Abu Hanifa – may God have mercy on him – was called Murji' because he deferred the matter of the major sinner to God's will, and 'irja' is deferment. It is narrated from him that it was said to him: 'From whom did you take irja?' He said: 'From the angels, peace be upon them, when they said: We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.' And the wicked Murji'a claimed that none of the Muslims would be punished for any major sin, and just as a good deed does not benefit with disbelief, so a bad deed does not harm with faith. This statement was narrated from Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the commentator.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents the orthodox Sunni view (Ahl al-Haqq) regarding a Muslim who commits a major sin. It first defines the person: one who commits a major sin without considering it lawful or treating it lightly, but due to overwhelming desire or zeal, and who still hopes for God's forgiveness and fears punishment. Such a person is called a believer; his faith remains intact and is not nullified. Faith is not lost except by the same door through which it was entered, i.e., by explicit disbelief. The ruling for such a person if he dies without repentance is that his fate is left to God's will: God may forgive him by His grace, or leave him with his faith and good deeds (perhaps meaning He does not punish him eternally), or allow intercession by righteous people. Alternatively, God may punish him in proportion to his sin, but ultimately he will enter Paradise and not remain in Hell eternally. The passage then addresses the label 'Murji'a' applied to Abu Hanifa because he deferred judgment of the major sinner to God's will. Abu Hanifa explained that he learned this deferral from the angels' statement in the Quran (Surah al-Baqarah 2:32). Finally, the passage refutes the deviant Murji'a who claim that no Muslim will be punished for any major sin, comparing this to the false notion that a bad deed does not harm with faith just as a good deed does not benefit with disbelief. This false view is attributed to Muqatil ibn Sulayman.

Key Terms
كبيرة
إيمان
مشيئة
إرجاء
مرجئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Ahl al-Haqq believe the major sinner is guaranteed forgiveness.
Correction: The passage teaches that forgiveness is subject to God's will; punishment may occur, but final outcome is Paradise.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Haqq view presented, what is the ultimate fate of a believer who dies unrepentant after committing a major sin?
A) He will be punished eternally in the Fire.
B) He will enter Paradise after possible punishment, but not remain in Hell forever.
C) He is considered a hypocrite and will be in the lowest level of Hell.
D) His faith nullifies the punishment completely.

Key Takeaway
A believer who commits a major sin remains a believer; his ultimate fate depends on God's will, but he will not remain in Hell forever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly feel both hope for God's forgiveness and fear of His punishment, as described in the passage? Which feeling dominates, and how does that affect your actions?
?
The passage teaches that the major sinner will not be eternally punished if he dies with faith. Does this make you complacent about sin, or does it drive you to repent? How would you counsel someone who uses this as an excuse to sin?
?
Abu Hanifa learned deferment of judgment from the angels' humble statement: 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.' In your daily interactions with other believers who sin, do you presume to know their final fate? How can you adopt the angels' humility in judging others?

Maturidi on the Minor Sinner
Maturidi states the rule for the minor sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on the minor sinner: that he remains a believer and, upon avoiding major sins, deserves forgiveness.

Core Idea
Minor sins do not expel from faith; avoiding major sins merits divine forgiveness.

Imam al-Maturidi's view on the minor sinner clarifies a key point in Islamic theology: sin does not necessarily negate faith. In this brief but dense passage, he outlines the relationship between minor sins, major sins, and the believer's status. Let's read his exact words and unpack their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the concise legal style of Imam al-Maturidi.
The passage is a single sentence but contains three key claims.
2
Identify the three claims
Find the three components: (1) the status of the minor sinner as a believer, (2) the condition of avoiding major sins, (3) the deserved forgiveness and that Allah will not punish him for the minor sins.
Each claim builds on the previous one.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Consider how this passage balances divine justice and mercy: sin does not automatically expel from faith, but forgiveness is tied to avoiding major sins.
This position distinguishes Maturidis from Kharijites and Mu'tazilites on the status of sinners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صاحب الصغيرة فهو عندهم مؤمن ولو اجتنب الكبائر لاستحق مغفرة الصغائر ولا يجوز الله تعذيبه عليها.
As for the one who commits a minor sin, he is with them a believer, and if he avoids major sins, he deserves forgiveness for the minor sins, and it is not permissible for God to punish him for them.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage defines the Maturidi position on the minor sinner. It states that the person who commits a minor sin remains a believer (مؤمن). If he avoids major sins (كبائر), he deserves forgiveness for his minor sins (صغائر) through God's mercy. Furthermore, it asserts that God cannot legitimately punish him solely for those minor sins. This reflects the Maturidi emphasis on divine justice and mercy, and the distinction between major and minor sins.

Key Terms
صاحب الصغيرة
الكبائر
الصغائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that minor sins are automatically forgiven without any condition.
Correction: The passage conditions forgiveness on avoiding major sins; it is not automatic.

Checkpoint

A) He becomes a disbeliever.
B) He deserves forgiveness for the minor sins.
C) He must be punished for the minor sins.
D) He is considered a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view affirms that a believer remains a believer despite minor sins, and forgiveness is promised for those who avoid major sins.

The Khawarij's Scriptural Case for Excommunicating Sinners
Khawarij present Quranic verses to equate sin with disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main Quranic proofs the Khawarij used to equate all sins with disbelief and see why this interpretation is controversial.

Core Idea
The Khawarij pressed the Quran's threats literally to claim any sin is disbelief.

The Khawarij were known for their strict position that any sin, large or small, makes a person a disbeliever. They did not rely on one verse alone but built a case from multiple passages. In this lesson, we will examine the scriptural evidence they put forward and see how they linked disobedience, eternal punishment, and the categories of believers and disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Khawarij's first proof
Start with the verse about disobeying God and His Messenger, and note how the Khawarij interpret 'eternal in fire' as applying to any disobedience. Reflect on the idea that all sins are equal under the term 'disobedience'.
2
Examine the other verses cited
Look at the verses about the fire prepared for disbelievers, the judgment verse (5:44), and the division into right and left. Ask yourself: Do these verses necessarily apply to all sinners?
The Khawarij assume a simple equation between sin and disbelief.
3
Consider the alternative view mentioned
The passage notes that some say the sinner becomes a polytheist (mushrik) rather than a disbeliever. Notice the counterargument about deed vs. speech.
4
Summarise the Khawarij's logic
Put together the chain: all sins are disobedience → disobedience leads to eternal fire → eternal fire is for disbelievers → therefore sinners are disbelievers. Identify where mainstream scholars would disagree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة الخوارج على قولهم]: ثم إن الخوارج القائلين بإكفار كل من وجد منه عصيان صغيرة كانت أو كبيرة تعلقوا بقوله تعالى : ( وَمَن يَعْصِ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ وَيَتَعَدَّ حُدُودَهُ يُدْخِلْهُ نَارًا خَلِدًا فيها ) من يعصيه يدخله نارا خالدًا فيها ثم قال : لَا يَصْلَنَهَا إِلَّا الْأَشْقَى الَّذِي كَذَّبَ وَتَوَلَّى والذنوب كلها في تحقيق اسم العصيان واحدة، ثم إن الله تعالى أخبر أن ﴿ وَاتَّقُوا النَّارَ الَّتي أُعِدَّتْ لِلْكَفِرِينَ ﴾ وقال : ﴿ وَمَن لَّمْ يَحْكُم بِمَا أَنزَلَ اللهُ فأولئكَ هُمُ الكَفِرُونَ ﴾ وقال : ﴿ فَأَمَّا مَنْ أُوتِيَ كِتَبُهُ بِيَمِينِهِ ﴾ وقال : ﴿ وَأَمَّا مَنْ أُوتِيَ كَتَبَهُ بِشِمَالِهِ ﴾ قسم الناس قسمين: أحدهما: أصحاب اليمين وهم المؤمنون، والثاني: أصحاب الشمال وهم الكافرون، والعاصي من أصحاب الشمال. والذين يقولون إنه يكون مشركاً لا كافرًا يزعمون أنه صار إلى ما صار بالفعل دون القول، وقد قال الله تعالى: فَمَن كَانَ يَرْجُو لِقَاءَ رَبِّهِ فَلْيَعْمَلْ عَمَلًا صَلِحَا وَلَا يُشْرِكْ بِعِبَادَةِ رَبِّهِ أَحَدًا.
[Proofs of the Khawarij for their statement]: Then the Khawarij, who declare as disbelievers everyone from whom disobedience is found, whether minor or major, clung to the saying of God Almighty: 'And whoever disobeys God and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will admit him to a Fire, abiding eternally therein.' He will admit the one who disobeys Him into a Fire, abiding eternally therein. Then He said: 'None will burn therein except the most wretched who denies and turns away.' And all sins are one in the realization of the term 'disobedience.' Then God Almighty informed that 'And fear the Fire which has been prepared for the disbelievers' and He said: 'And whoever does not judge by what God has revealed, then those are the disbelievers' and He said: 'So as for he who is given his book in his right hand' and He said: 'But as for he who is given his book in his left hand.' He divided people into two categories: one: the companions of the right, and they are the believers; the second: the companions of the left, and they are the disbelievers; and the disobedient one is among the companions of the left. And those who say that he becomes a polytheist, not a disbeliever, claim that he has arrived at what he arrived at by deed, not by speech. And God Almighty said: 'So whoever hopes for the meeting with his Lord, let him do righteous work and not associate anyone in the worship of his Lord.'
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural proofs used by the Khawarij to support their position that any disobedience, whether minor or major, constitutes disbelief and merits eternal punishment. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, they cite the verse 'And whoever disobeys God and His Messenger... He will admit him to a Fire, abiding eternally therein' (Quran 4:14), which they interpret as applying to any disobedience. They then cite verse 92:15-16, which says that only the most wretched who denies and turns away will burn in the Fire. The Khawarij argue that since all sins share the common quality of being 'disobedience' (عصيان), they are all equally deserving of the threat. Next, they cite verses that describe the Fire as prepared for disbelievers (3:131) and that those who do not judge by God's revelation are disbelievers (5:44). The Khawarij also use the division of people into the companions of the right (believers) and the companions of the left (disbelievers) from Surah al-Inshiqaq and al-Haqqah, arguing that the disobedient are among the latter. Finally, they address those who say the sinner becomes a polytheist (مشرك) rather than a disbeliever, claiming that this status is achieved by action alone, and they quote the verse about not associating partners in worship (18:110) to support the idea that sin can be a form of shirk. The passage shows the Khawarij's reliance on literal interpretation of Quranic threats and their rejection of distinctions between sin types.

Key Terms
عصيان
كافر
مشرك
أصحاب اليمين
أصحاب الشمال
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khawarij rely on a single verse to prove their point.
Correction: The passage shows they use multiple verses (4:14, 92:15-16, 3:131, 5:44, and the division of people) to build their case.

Checkpoint
What is the key reasoning the Khawarij use to equate all sins?
A) All sins are major.
B) All sins share the common term 'disobedience' (عصيان).
C) The verse only mentions major sins.
D) They ignore minor sins.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij's case for excommunicating sinners rested on a literal and undifferentiated reading of Quranic threats, disregarding other verses about mercy and forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever written someone off as a bad Muslim (or worse) because of a sin you observed, forgetting that you yourself are constantly dependent on Allah's mercy? How can you guard your heart against the Khawarij's harshness while still hating the sin?
?
When you read Quranic threats like 'Whoever disobeys God... will be in Fire eternally,' do you feel fear that drives you to despair, or hope that leads you to repentance? Which emotion dominates your relationship with Allah, and is it balanced by the verses of mercy you may be ignoring?
?
The Khawarij used sacred texts to justify excommunicating and even killing Muslims. Is there any area in your life where you use your religious knowledge to distance yourself from others, feeling superior or self-righteous? What would it take for you to replace that attitude with the prophetic example of mercy?

Hasan al-Basri's Proof: Major Sin as Hypocrisy
Hasan al-Basri's scriptural and logical proof that the major sinner is a hypocrite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Hasan al-Basri used the contradiction between verbal faith and sinful actions, supported by Quran and hadith, to argue that committing major sins makes one a hypocrite.

Core Idea
Major sin contradicts faith, making it hypocrisy, not disbelief.

Hasan al-Basri offers a distinctive argument that a major sinner is not an unbeliever but a hypocrite, because they affirm faith verbally while violating it in action. This lesson unpacks his scriptural evidence and logical extension from the three signs of a hypocrite to all major sins.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of hypocrisy
Read the opening statement: hypocrisy is the contradiction between verbal profession of faith and actual deeds.
Hasan al-Basri defines hypocrisy as acting against the limits of faith.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
Look at the verse from Surah al-Ankabut (29:1-3) which indicates that faith is tested to distinguish truthful from liars.
The verse shows that verbal claim is not enough; actions must align.
3
Analyze the hadith on three signs
Identify the three signs of a hypocrite mentioned by the Prophet: betrayal of trust, lying, and breaking promises.
These are specific examples of contradiction between words and deeds.
4
See the logical extension
Understand that other sins are equal to these three in being acts of disobedience, so they also necessitate the name and ruling of hypocrisy.
This extends the ruling to all major sins that contradict faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل الحسن البصري]: ووجه قول الحسن أنه خالف بفعله ما أعطى بلسانه من الإيمان وحفظ حدوده، وتعاهد ذلك وهذا هو النفاق. قال الله تعالى : ( الر ) أَحَسِبَ النَّاسُ أَن يُتْرَكُوا أَن يَقُولُوا امَنَا وَهُمْ لَا يُفْتَنُونَ وَلَقَدْ فَتَنَّا الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ فَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ صَدَقُوا وَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ الْكَذِبِينَ ويدل عليه ما روى عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : علامة المنافق ثلاث إذا أؤتمن خان وإذا حدث كذب وإذا وعد أخلف وغير هذه الأشياء الثلاثة يساويها في كونها عصيانا فيساويها في إيجاب اسم النفاق وحكمه لمرتكبها.
[Proof of al-Hasan al-Basri]: The import of al-Hasan's statement is that he has, by his action, contradicted what he affirmed with his tongue of faith and its limits, and his commitment to that; and this is hypocrisy. God Almighty said: 'Alif, Lam, Ra. Do people think that they will be left to say, "We believe," and they will not be tried? And We have certainly tried those before them, and God will surely know those who are truthful and will surely know the liars.' And it is indicated by what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'The signs of a hypocrite are three: when he is entrusted, he betrays; when he speaks, he lies; and when he promises, he breaks his promise.' And other things besides these three are equal to them in being disobedience, so they are equal to them in necessitating the name and ruling of hypocrisy for the one who commits them.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural and hadith-based argument of Hasan al-Basri for considering the major sinner a hypocrite. The reasoning is that the major sinner, by committing a grave sin, contradicts the faith he professes verbally and violates the limits (حدود) of faith he committed to uphold. This contradiction between word and deed is the essence of hypocrisy (نفاق). Hasan al-Basri supports this with the Quranic verse from Surah al-Ankabut (29:1-3) which says that people are tested to distinguish the truthful from the liars in their claim of faith. He then cites a well-known hadith in which the Prophet enumerates three signs of a hypocrite: betraying trust, lying in speech, and breaking promises. Hasan al-Basri extends this argument by saying that other sins, being acts of disobedience, are equivalent to these three signs in terms of disobedience; therefore, they also necessitate the label and ruling of hypocrisy for the one who commits them. This shows a principle that any act of disobedience that contradicts one's verbal faith amounts to hypocrisy, not just outright disbelief.

Key Terms
النفاق
المنافق
الإيمان
حدوده
العلامات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins make a person a hypocrite.
Correction: Hasan al-Basri only considers major sins that contradict one's faith as hypocrisy, not minor sins or sins from weakness.

Checkpoint
According to Hasan al-Basri's proof, why is the major sinner considered a hypocrite?
A) He denies God entirely.
B) His actions contradict the faith he professes verbally.
C) He only commits minor sins.
D) He does not believe in the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Hasan al-Basri argues that committing major sins transforms one into a hypocrite because it contradicts the faith one verbally professes, as supported by Quranic testing and prophetic signs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you recognize that your action contradicts the faith you profess with your tongue? What does this contradiction reveal about the state of your iman right now? Will you repent immediately today and seek to align your actions with your words?
?
The Prophet (PBUH) listed three signs of hypocrisy: betraying trust, lying, and breaking promises. Examine your own life: in which of these three do you fall short? What specific change will you make starting today to remove this trait from your character?
?
Hasan al-Basri argues that any major sin is equivalent to those three signs in making one a hypocrite. If you were to be tested today as the Quran describes, would you be among the truthful or the liars? Why? And what concrete step will you take to ensure your actions match your faith from this moment forward?

Faith and Disbelief

6 min
The Grave Sinner Remains a Believer (Refuting the Intermediate State)
Nasafi proves that the grave sinner is not a kafir or munafiq and refutes the manzila bayna manzilatayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's proof that a person who commits a grave sin remains a believer because faith is affirmation (tasdiq) which is not negated by sin, and why the Mu'tazila doctrine of an intermediate state is impossible.

Core Idea
Sin without denial does not remove faith; affirmation and denial are mutually exclusive.

This lesson presents Imam al-Nasafi's rational proof that a believer who commits a grave sin does not lose faith. He argues that faith is inner affirmation (tasdiq), which is only undone by denial (takdhib). Since a sinner may commit sins without denying the truth in his heart, he remains a believer. Nasafi also refutes the Mu'tazila concept of a middle state between faith and disbelief, showing it is logically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic source and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice how Nasafi uses compact language.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on tasdiq (affirmation), takdhib (denial), fisq (transgression), and nifaq (hypocrisy). Understand their definitions as used by Nasafi.
3
Follow the logical steps
Nasafi uses an analogy (standing/sitting) and then argues that there is no intermediate between affirmation and denial. Then he addresses the nature of sin as action, not denial.
Pay attention to how he refutes the intermediate state.
4
Understand the refutation of hypocrisy
Nasafi explains that true hypocrisy is inner corruption, while a sinner with sound faith is not a hypocrite.
This clarifies a common misunderstanding.
5
Connect to the sectarian debate
Recognize that this argument directly opposes the Mu'tazila position of manzila bayna manzilatayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل لنا أن الإيمان هو التصديق وضده التكذيب فما لم يتبدل التصديق بالتكذيب بقى الذات مؤمنًا، كما أن القيام ما لم يتبدل بالقعود بقى الذات قائما، وكذا لا واسطة بين التصديق والتكذيب إلا الارتياب وهو كفر أيضا، فلم يكن بين الإيمان والكفر واسطة فالقول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين محال غير معقول، والفسق في اللغة عبارة عن الخروج فمن خرج عن ائتمار أمر من أوامر الله تعالى يكون فاسقا، وكذا العصيان عبارة عن مخالفته الأمر فعلا لا عن الجحود والتكذيب، وليس من ضرورة مخالفة الأمر أو الخروج عن الائتمار التكذيب إذ الخلاف بيننا وبين خصومنا في هذا الفاسق الذي لم يأتمر لبعض الأوامر لا جحودًا ولا استخفافا بمن أمر بل لكسل أو حمية أو أنفة أو غلبة شهوة أو رجاء عفو، ولا مضادة بين الخروج عن الائتمار ومخالفة الأمر على هذا الوجه وبين التصديق، فلم يكن من ضرورة حصولها انعدام التصديق بل بقي التصديق ثابتا بلا خلاف بيننا وبين الخصوم، إذ الخلاف فيه وما دام التصديق باقيًا كان التكذيب منعدما فالقول بكفره والتكذيب منعدم أو بزوال الإيمان والتصديق قائم أو بثبوت النفاق والتصديق في القلب متكرر، ظاهر الفساد بين الخطأ، يحققه أن النفاق المعروف هو إظهار فساد الباطن فمن جعل من صلحت سريرته وظهر منه فسق في ظاهره مع صلاح السريرة مع منافقا فقد قلب القصة وغير الحقيقة، وهو في غاية البطلان.
The proof for us is that faith is affirmation (al-tasdiq) and its opposite is denial (al-takdhib). As long as affirmation is not replaced by denial, the person remains a believer, just as standing, as long as it is not replaced by sitting, the person remains standing. Likewise, there is no intermediate between affirmation and denial except doubt (al-irtiyab), which is also unbelief (kufr). Thus there is no intermediate between faith and unbelief, so the statement of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn) is impossible and unintelligible. And fisq in language is an expression for exiting: whoever exits from compliance with an order of the commands of God Almighty is a fasiq. Likewise, 'isyan is an expression for opposing His command in action, not for denial and rejection. It is not necessary that opposing the command or exiting from compliance entails denial, because the disagreement between us and our opponents concerns this fasiq who did not comply with some commands not out of denial nor out of contempt for the commander, but out of laziness, zeal, pride, overwhelming desire, or hope of pardon. There is no contradiction between exiting from compliance and opposing the command in this manner and affirmation. So it is not necessary that their occurrence entails the absence of affirmation; rather affirmation remains established without disagreement between us and the opponents, since the disagreement is about it. As long as affirmation remains, denial is absent. So the statement that he is an unbeliever while denial is absent, or that his faith has ceased while affirmation is present, or that hypocrisy (nifaq) is established while affirmation is repeatedly in the heart, is clearly corrupt and erroneous. He confirms it by [saying]: known hypocrisy is the display of the corruption of the inner self. Whoever makes the one whose inner self is sound but outward fisq appears from him, along with sound inner self, a hypocrite, has turned the story upside down and changed the reality, and that is extreme falsehood.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a proof from Imam al-Nasafi defending the Sunni position that a person who commits a grave sin (kabira) remains a believer as long as he has not denied the truth in his heart. The author begins by stating that faith (iman) is tasdiq (affirmation) and its opposite is takdhib (denial). Just as a person who is standing remains standing until he sits, a person remains a believer as long as he does not replace affirmation with denial. There is no intermediate state between affirmation and denial except doubt (irtiyab), which itself is a form of denial, so faith and unbelief are mutually exclusive. The Mu'tazila doctrine of al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn (the intermediate position) is therefore impossible. The author then explains that the terms fisq (transgression) and 'isyan (disobedience) refer, in language, to exiting from compliance or opposing a command in action, not to denial. A person may commit a sin without denying the command—he may be lazy, proud, overwhelmed by desire, or hoping for forgiveness. Such actions do not contradict the inward affirmation (tasdiq). Therefore, the sinful believer retains his faith; to call him an unbeliever or a hypocrite is absurd, because he still affirms the truth. The passage ends by emphasizing that genuine hypocrisy (nifaq) is the display of corruption inwardly, whereas the person with sound inward faith and outward sin is not a hypocrite.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
التكذيب
الارتياب
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
الفسق
العصيان
النفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that committing a grave sin automatically makes one a non-believer or a hypocrite.
Correction: Sin in action does not remove inner affirmation; only denial in heart removes faith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the intermediate position (manzila bayna manzilatayn) impossible?
A) Because there is no term for such a position.
B) Because there is no intermediate between affirmation and denial except doubt, which is itself disbelief.
C) Because the Mu'tazila rejected it.
D) Because the Quran explicitly denies it.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that a grave sinner remains a believer because faith is inner affirmation, which is not negated by sin committed without denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin due to laziness or desire, do you feel your faith is weakened or lost? What does Nasafi's argument demand you believe about your own iman in that moment? Will you commit to treating your sin as a failure of action, not of faith, and immediately repent without despairing of your belief?
?
This passage says there is no middle state between faith and disbelief. Have you ever lived as if there is a 'safe gray zone'—sinning casually while assuming faith still remains? What one step can you take today to make your affirmation more conscious and active, eliminating that illusion?
?
How often do you judge others who commit major sins as having 'no faith' or being hypocrites? After reading this passage, can you honestly see their inner affirmation might be intact? Will you change how you speak about and treat them, recognizing that only Allah knows the heart?

Proving Faith as Affirmation from Practice and Language
Nasafi enumerates practical evidence and linguistic proof that faith is tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses practical consensus and linguistic definition to prove that faith is inner affirmation (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Faith is tasdiq because the Muslim community treats all Muslims as believers in practice.

Nasafi presents a robust argument that faith is not merely outward profession but inner affirmation. He points to the universal practices of Muslims—like marriage, eating slaughtered meat, and congregational worship—which do not exclude grave sinners, showing they are considered believers. Then he adds linguistic evidence. This passage establishes the basis for the Ahl al-Sunna view that grave sinners remain believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of practices Nasafi mentions.
The passage begins with 'ثم دعوانا'.
2
Identify the practical evidences
List the practices Nasafi cites: proofs of Quran, consensus of Muslims, transactions, congregations, gatherings of dhikr, permissibility of slaughter and marriage. Note that these do not distinguish between grave sinners and others.
These practices form the practical consensus.
3
Understand the linguistic point
Nasafi adds that linguists only know iman as tasdiq. This reinforces the argument that faith is affirmation.
4
Grasp the conclusion
From all this, Nasafi concludes that judging a grave sinner as unbeliever or hypocrite is wrong, because it asserts denial where there is affirmation, and denies affirmation where it exists.
This is the key rebuttal of excommunication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم دعوانا أن الإيمان هو التصديق فلأن الذي ثبت بأدلة القرآن وما عليه إجماع أهل الإسلام وجري المعاملات والاشتراك في الجماعات والاجتماع في مجالس الذكر وحل الذبيحة وجواز المناكحة : أن الإيمان هو تصديق الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم بما جاء به من عند الله تعالى، إذ هذه الأمور كلها مبنية عليه بلا خلاف بيننا وبين الخصوم، ولا يميزون فيها بين من ارتكب كبيرة وبين من لم يرتكب، فثبت أن الإيمان هذا هو عند الكل، مع أن أهل اللغة لا يعرفون الإيمان إلا التصديق، فإذا كان الأمر كذلك ثبت أن من حكم بكفر صاحب الكبيرة أو بنفاقه أو بخروجه عن الإيمان كان مخطئًا قاضيًا بوجود ما هو المنعدم وهو التكذيب، وبعدم ما هو الموجود وهو التصديق، وفساد هذا ظاهر ثم ما تزعم المعتزلة أن إطلاق اسم الفسق مجمع عليه وإطلاق ما وراء ذلك من اسم المؤمن والكافر والمنافق مختلف فيه فأخذنا بالمجمع عليه وتركنا المختلف فيه. كلام باطل، إذ هو أخذ بالمجمع عليه وأعرض عن المخالفة،
Then our claim that faith is affirmation is because what is established by the proofs of the Quran, the consensus of the people of Islam, the conduct of transactions, participation in congregations, gathering in sessions of remembrance, permitting slaughter, and allowing marriage, is that faith is the affirmation of the Messenger (peace be upon him) in what he brought from God Almighty. For all these matters are built upon it without disagreement between us and the opponents, and they do not distinguish in them between one who committed a grave sin and one who did not. Thus it is established that faith is this in the view of all. Moreover, the people of language know faith only as affirmation. So if the matter is thus, it is established that whoever judges the grave sinner to be an unbeliever, a hypocrite, or out of faith is erring, judging the existence of what is absent—denial—and the absence of what is present—affirmation. The corruption of this is apparent. Then what the Mu'tazila claim—that the term fasiq is universally agreed upon, while the terms believer, unbeliever, and hypocrite beyond that are disputed, so we take what is agreed upon and leave what is disputed—is false speech. For it is taking the agreed upon while turning away from the disagreement.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a dual argument: first, a proof that iman is tasdiq based on practical consensus and linguistic usage; second, a refutation of a Mu'tazila objection. The author begins by stating that our claim that faith is affirmation (tasdiq) is supported by multiple indicators: the Qur'anic proofs, the consensus of Muslims, the way transactions are conducted, participation in congregational worship, attending gatherings of dhikr, the permissibility of eating the meat of a person's slaughter, and the validity of marriage. All these practices are built upon the assumption that the person is a believer (through affirmation), and there is no distinction between one who commits a grave sin and one who does not. Thus, the universal practice of Muslims treats the grave sinner as a believer. Additionally, linguists define iman as tasdiq. Therefore, anyone who calls a grave sinner an unbeliever or a hypocrite is making an error, because they affirm the absence of denial (which is absent) and deny the presence of affirmation (which is present). The author then addresses the Mu'tazila argument that there is consensus on calling the grave sinner a fasiq, and the other terms (mu'min, kafir, munafiq) are disputed, so we should stick with the agreed term (fasiq) and avoid dispute. Nasafi dismisses this as false, because the method of taking the agreed term while ignoring the dispute is not proper: the Mu'tazila are themselves taking a position that contradicts the consensus on the fundamental status of the sinner as a believer in practice. The argument is that the practical consensus (e.g., treating the sinner as a believer in transactions) is stronger than a verbal label.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
الإجماع
أهل اللغة
الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main basis for Nasafi's argument that grave sinners are still believers?
A) The Mu'tazila agreed to it.
B) The practical practices of Muslims, such as marriage and slaughter, do not distinguish between grave sinners and others.
C) The Qur'an explicitly states that all sinners are believers.
D) There is no such thing as a grave sin.

Key Takeaway
Faith is defined as affirmation (tasdiq) based on universal Muslim practice and language, and this is the criterion for judging believer status, not sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a Muslim committing a major sin, do you internally regard them as a believer or do you feel they have lost their faith? How does this lesson challenge your heart attitude toward them?
?
Have you ever withheld brotherly treatment (like greeting, eating together, or marrying) from someone because of their sins? What would it cost you to treat them as a believer despite their failures?
?
If you truly believed that faith is solely affirmation, would you still fear for your own salvation because of your sins? How does this truth liberate you from despair or, conversely, remove excuses for neglecting deeds?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Claim on Consensus about Fasiq
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim of consensus on applying the term fasiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects the Mu'tazila argument that there is consensus on calling grave sinners 'fasiq' and that this label should be used.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claim of consensus on fasiq ignores the stronger practical consensus treating sinners as believers.

In this passage, Nasafi directly addresses a Mu'tazila objection. They argue that since there is consensus on calling a grave sinner 'fasiq' (sinner) but disagreement on other labels, we should stick with the agreed term. Nasafi refutes this by pointing out that the practical consensus (what Muslims do) treats the sinner as a believer, and that is more fundamental. The Mu'tazila method of taking the agreed term while ignoring the underlying disagreement is flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
The passage is short. Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the Mu'tazila argument and Nasafi's response.
The passage begins with 'ثم ما تزعم المعتزلة'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila claim there is consensus on calling a grave sinner 'fasiq', while terms like believer, unbeliever, hypocrite are disputed. So they argue we should use 'fasiq' to avoid dispute.
3
Grasp Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi dismisses this as false because it takes the agreed term while ignoring the disagreement about the sinner's underlying status. The practical consensus (from the previous lesson) treats the sinner as a believer, so the Mu'tazila are ignoring that.
The falseness is that they claim to take the agreed but actually ignore the real agreement.

Thus it is established that faith is this in the view of all. Moreover, the people of language know faith only as affirmation. So if the matter is thus, it is established that whoever judges the grave sinner to be an unbeliever, a hypocrite, or out of faith is erring, judging the existence of what is absent—denial—and the absence of what is present—affirmation. The corruption of this is apparent. Then what the Mu'tazila claim—that the term fasiq is universally agreed upon, while the terms believer, unbeliever, and hypocrite beyond that are disputed, so we take what is agreed upon and leave what is disputed—is false speech. For it is taking the agreed upon while turning away from the disagreement.

Commentary
This passage is a dual argument: first, a proof that iman is tasdiq based on practical consensus and linguistic usage; second, a refutation of a Mu'tazila objection. The author begins by stating that our claim that faith is affirmation (tasdiq) is supported by multiple indicators: the Qur'anic proofs, the consensus of Muslims, the way transactions are conducted, participation in congregational worship, attending gatherings of dhikr, the permissibility of eating the meat of a person's slaughter, and the validity of marriage. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe there is consensus on calling grave sinners 'fasiq', so that should be the only term used.
Correction: Nasafi shows that this ignores the practical consensus that treats sinners as believers, making the Mu'tazila claim false.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the Mu'tazila argument about calling grave sinners 'fasiq'?
A) Because the term 'fasiq' is not agreed upon at all.
B) Because the Mu'tazila are taking an agreed term but ignoring the deeper disagreement about the sinner's status.
C) Because Nasafi agrees with the Mu'tazila.
D) Because the Qur'an forbids the term 'fasiq'.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument about consensus on 'fasiq' is invalid because it ignores the stronger practical consensus that grave sinners are believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement among scholars, do you tend to pick the view that seems easiest or most agreed upon, even if it means ignoring deeper issues that challenge your comfort? How can you train yourself to confront the full reality of a dispute rather than taking shortcuts?
?
This lesson shows the danger of cherry-picking consensus to avoid uncomfortable theological commitments. In your own life, are there areas where you selectively appeal to 'what everyone agrees on' to avoid facing a harder truth about your beliefs or actions? What would it mean for you to honestly examine both the agreement and the disagreement?

Iman is Tasdiq: The Evidence from Practice and Language
Al-Nasafi provides evidence for the definition of iman as tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Nasafi argues that iman is tasdiq based on Quran, consensus, practical Islamic matters, and linguistic evidence, and that this definition excludes no believer, even major sinners.

Core Idea
Iman is inner assent (tasdiq), proven by scripture, consensus, and everyday Islamic practice.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents the positive case for defining iman as tasdiq. He draws on multiple sources of evidence—Quranic proofs, the consensus of Muslims, and the way Islamic laws are applied universally to all who profess belief, regardless of their sins. This reinforces the Maturidi position that iman is a single, unchanging assent that is not diminished by disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the core claim and its evidence
The passage begins with 'Then our claim that iman is tasdiq is because...' Read this section carefully to see the types of evidence al-Nasafi presents.
2
Identify the types of evidence
Al-Nasafi lists several proofs: Quranic evidence, consensus of Muslims, and practical matters such as transactions, congregational prayers, gatherings for remembrance, permissibility of slaughtered meat, and marriage. Note each one.
These are all based on the person being a believer.
3
Notice the practical implication
These practices are applied equally to those who commit major sins and those who do not. This shows that major sins do not affect the believer's status in these matters.
4
Understand the linguistic evidence
Al-Nasafi points out that in the Arabic language, iman means only tasdiq. This supports the definition.
5
See the conclusion
Anyone who judges a major sinner as a disbeliever or hypocrite is contradicting reality—they treat tasdiq as absent when it is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمر بل لكسل أو حمية أو أنفة أو غلبة شهوة أو رجاء عفو، ولا مضادة بين الخروج عن الائتمار ومخالفة الأمر على هذا الوجه وبين التصديق، فلم يكن من ضرورة حصولها انعدام التصديق بل بقي التصديق ثابتا بلا خلاف بيننا وبين الخصوم، إذ الخلاف فيه وما دام التصديق باقيا كان التكذيب منعدما فالقول بكفره والتكذيب منعدم أو بزوال الإيمان والتصديق قائم أو بثبوت النفاق والتصديق في القلب متكرر، ظاهر الفساد بين الخطأ، يحققه أن النفاق المعروف هو إظهار فساد الباطن فمن جعل من صلحت سريرته وظهر منه فسق في ظاهره الصلاح مع منافقا فقد قلب القصة وغير الحقيقة، وهو في غاية البطلان. [الإيمان هو التصديق]: ثم دعوانا أن الإيمان هوا التصديق فلأن الذي ثبت بأدلة القرآن وما عليه إجماع أهل الإسلام وجري المعاملات والاشتراك في الجماعات والاجتماع في مجالس الذكر وحل الذبيحة وجواز المناكحة : أن الإيمان هو تصديق الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم بما جاء به من عند الله تعالى، إذ هذه الأمور كلها مبنية عليه بلا خلاف بيننا وبين الخصوم، ولا يميزون فيها بين من ارتكب كبيرة وبين من لم يرتكب، فثبت أن الإيمان هذا هو عند الكل، مع أن أهل اللغة لا يعرفون الإيمان إلا التصديق، فإذا كان الأمر كذلك ثبت أن من حكم بكفر صاحب الكبيرة أو بنفاقه أو بخروجه عن الإيمان كان مخطئا قاضيا بوجود ما هو المنعدم وهو التكذيب، وبعدم ما هو الموجود وهو التصديق، وفساد هذا ظاهر
Command, but due to laziness, zeal, pride, overwhelming desire, or hope of pardon; and there is no contradiction between leaving compliance and disobeying the command in this manner and [having] tasdiq (assent). So it is not necessary that the occurrence of these [sins] entails the negation of tasdiq; rather tasdiq remains established without dispute between us and the opponents. And as long as tasdiq remains, takdhib (disbelief/denial) is absent. So the statement that [the major sinner] is a kafir while takdhib is absent, or that iman has departed while tasdiq is present, or that hypocrisy is established while tasdiq is in the heart, is clearly corrupt and erroneous. This is confirmed by the fact that the known hypocrisy is the manifestation of inner corruption. So whoever considers one whose inner state is sound but from whom outward immorality appears, and [calls] him a hypocrite, has reversed the matter and altered the reality, and this is utterly false. [Iman is tasdiq]: Then our claim that iman is tasdiq is because what is established by the proofs of the Quran, the consensus of the people of Islam, and the practice of transactions, shared participation in congregational [prayers], gathering in assemblies of remembrance, permissibility of slaughtered meat, and permissibility of marriage, is that iman is tasdiq of the Messenger (peace be upon him) regarding what he brought from Allah Almighty. For all these matters are based upon it without dispute between us and the opponents, and they do not distinguish in these matters between one who commits a major sin and one who does not. So it is established that this iman is [agreed upon] by everyone. Moreover, the linguists know iman only as tasdiq. Since the matter is thus, it is established that whoever judges the major sinner as a kafir, a hypocrite, or as having left iman is mistaken — he judges as existent what is non-existent, namely takdhib, and as non-existent what is existent, namely tasdiq. The corruption of this is clear.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof that iman is tasdiq (assent of the heart) and a refutation of those who declare major sinners to be unbelievers or hypocrites. It begins by noting that sin (disobedience) does not contradict tasdiq: a person may sin out of laziness, pride, desire, etc., but this does not negate their inner assent. Since tasdiq remains, takdhib (denial) is absent, so calling such a person a kafir, claiming their iman has left, or labeling them a hypocrite is false. The author then confirms that the known meaning of hypocrisy is inner corruption, so one with sound inner belief cannot be a hypocrite just because of outward sin. Then the author states the central claim: iman is tasdiq. He supports this with multiple proofs: Quranic evidence, consensus of Muslims, and practical matters like transactions, congregational prayer, slaughter, and marriage — all of which are based on the person being a believer (i.e., having tasdiq). Significantly, these practices do not differentiate between a sinner and a non-sinner. Finally, he invokes linguistic evidence that iman means tasdiq. The conclusion is that anyone who declares a major sinner a kafir or munafiq is judging contrary to reality — he pretends tasdiq is absent when it is present.

Key Terms
إيمان
تصديق
تكذيب
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That iman is only verbal profession or that major sins remove iman entirely.
Correction: Iman is tasdiq (inner assent), and practice shows that even major sinners are treated as believers in all Islamic rulings; thus sins do not remove iman.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed by al-Nasafi as evidence for iman being tasdiq?
A) Quranic proofs
B) Consensus of Muslims
C) The practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him)
D) The permissibility of marriage

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi proves that iman is tasdiq through Quran, consensus, Islamic practice, and language, establishing that even major sinners remain believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever subtly think less of a Muslim who commits a major sin, doubting the sincerity of their faith? This lesson confronts you: will you now actively remind yourself that their tasdiq remains, even when they fall short, and treat them as a brother or sister in Islam?
?
Consider your own heart: when you commit a sin, do you fear that you have lost your iman, or do you recognize that your inner assent (tasdiq) is intact, though your actions need repentance? Will you let this truth bring you to sincere tawbah rather than despair?
?
The Prophet's community applied rulings of brotherhood to all believers, even those with major sins. Are you willing to extend that same unconditional brotherhood to every person who professes faith, no matter their outward state? Make a firm intention today to check your heart against takfir and arrogance.

The Major Sinner Remains a Believer: Sin Does Not Negate Tasdiq
Al-Nasafi explains why major sins do not remove iman and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's argument that committing major sins does not contradict or remove the tasdiq (assent) that constitutes iman, thereby refuting claims of kufr or nifaq.

Core Idea
Sins, even major ones, do not negate inner assent; a believer remains a believer.

Al-Nasafi begins by addressing the objection that major sins might invalidate iman. He clarifies that sins often arise from human weaknesses like laziness, pride, or desire, and these do not conflict with the inner assent of the heart. Since tasdiq remains, the person cannot be called a disbeliever or a hypocrite. He then reinforces this by defining hypocrisy correctly as inner corruption, not outward sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
The passage starts by stating that sins do not negate tasdiq. Read carefully the list of reasons for sin: laziness, zeal, pride, desire, hope of pardon.
2
Understand the relationship between sin and tasdiq
Al-Nasafi argues that sin and tasdiq are not contradictory; a person can have inner assent while committing outward disobedience.
This is key to the Maturidi position.
3
Follow the logical sequence
Since tasdiq remains, takdhib (denial) is absent. Therefore, calling the sinner a kafir, saying iman has left, or calling him a hypocrite is false.
Al-Nasafi refutes three positions explicitly.
4
Clarify the meaning of hypocrisy
Al-Nasafi defines hypocrisy as inner corruption manifested outwardly. If one's inner state is sound (tasdiq), then outward sin alone does not make one a hypocrite.

?

Quranic Proof that Iman Remains Despite Major Sins
Al-Nasafi cites three Quranic verses as proof that iman remains despite major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi uses Quranic verses to prove that the name of iman remains with believers even when they commit major sins.

Core Idea
Allah still calls sinners 'believers' in the Quran — iman remains.

Al-Nasafi now presents a direct Quranic proof for the Ahl al-Sunna position that major sins do not remove iman. He cites three verses where Allah addresses sinners as 'believers,' showing that the name of iman persists despite sin. This lesson examines those verses and the proof they provide.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the passage begins with 'then the proof' — it is part of a larger discussion.
2
Identify the three verses
Locate each verse al-Nasafi cites: 4:43 (drunkenness), 49:9 (fighting and oppression), and 2:178 (retaliation). For each, note the major sin mentioned.
The third verse (2:178) is noted to have a threefold indication, but the full list is not provided due to a page break.
3
Understand the logical inference
Al-Nasafi argues that since Allah preserves the address 'O you who have believed' for sinners, their iman must remain intact. If iman were removed, Allah would not call them believers.
This refutes the Kharijite view that major sins nullify iman.
4
Reflect on the strength of the proof
Consider why this is a powerful argument: it is based on the Quran's own wording, not human reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على صحة ما ذهب إليه أهل الحق أن الله تعالى أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجود ما عليه الوعيد بقوله تعالى: ﴿ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا لَا تَقْرَبُوا الصِّلَوْةَ وَأَنتُمْ سُكَرَى ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا ﴾ أبقى لهما اسم الإيمان مع أن أحديهما باغية، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ يَاأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ ﴾ وفي الآية دلالة من ثلاثة أوجه:
Then the proof for the correctness of what the People of Truth (ahl al-haqq) hold is that Allah Almighty preserved the name of iman despite the existence of what incurs the threat of punishment, by His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not approach prayer while you are drunk' (4:43), and His saying: 'And if two groups of believers fight, then make peace between them' (49:9) — He preserved for them the name of iman despite one of them being oppressive — and His saying: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is retaliation' (2:178). And in this verse there is indication from three aspects:
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage provides a Quranic proof for the Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth, i.e., the Ahl al-Sunna) view that major sins do not remove the name of iman. The proof is that Allah continues to address sinners as 'believers' even when they commit acts that invite His threat of punishment. The author cites three Quranic verses: (1) 'O you who have believed, do not approach prayer while you are drunk' — this addresses believers despite the major sin of drunkenness. (2) 'And if two groups of believers fight, then make peace between them' — Allah calls them 'believers' even as they fight, and one party is oppressive (baghiya). (3) 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is retaliation' — this addresses believers who may commit murder, a major sin. The author then notes that the last verse (2:178) contains a threefold indication (dalaala min thalathati awjuh), but the text ends before listing these three aspects. The argument is clear: preserving the name 'iman' for sinners shows that their iman remains intact despite their sins.

Key Terms
إيمان
مؤمن
آية
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These Quranic verses refer only to believers who are not actually committing sin, or that the sin temporarily removes iman.
Correction: The verses explicitly mention sins: drunkenness, oppression, and murder. Allah still calls them 'believers,' proving that iman persists even during the commission of major sins.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does al-Nasafi cite to show that Allah calls fighting believers 'believers' even when they are oppressive?
A) 4:43 (do not approach prayer while drunk)
B) 49:9 (if two groups of believers fight)
C) 2:178 (retaliation is prescribed)
D) 5:38 (cut off the hand of the thief)

Key Takeaway
Allah's preservation of the name 'believers' for sinners in the Quran proves that iman is not eradicated by major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have committed a major sin. Did you believe Allah no longer considers you a believer? How will this lesson's proof that He still calls you 'O you who have believed' change your relationship with Him today? Will you repent with hope or with shame?
?
Think of someone you know who openly commits a sin. Have you ever thought they are no longer a believer? Based on these verses, will you revise your judgment? What might that mean for how you treat them?
?
If iman remains despite major sins, does that make you less concerned about sinning? Or does it increase your gratitude and drive to avoid sin? What is one major sin you will consciously strive to leave starting today, knowing that your iman is from Allah's mercy, not your perfection?

Qur'anic Proof That Major Sins Do Not Negate Imān
Presents a Qur'anic proof that Allah preserves the name of imān despite major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an itself demonstrates that committing major sins does not remove a person from the fold of faith.

Core Idea
The Qur'an addresses sinners as believers, proving faith survives major sins.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a compelling Qur'anic argument that the People of Truth (Ahl al-Sunnah) rely on: despite committing grave sins like murder or drunkenness, the Qur'an continues to call such people 'believers.' This lesson examines the verses and the three-fold proof from the qisas verse, showing that iman remains intact even when one falls into major sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author lists several Qur'anic verses where sinners are addressed as 'believers.'
Pay attention to the three aspects derived from Surah al-Baqara 2:178.
2
Identify the key verses
The passage cites verses about drunkenness (4:43), fighting believers (49:9), and legal retribution (2:178). Reflect on why each verse is relevant to the issue of major sins and iman.
The verse on qisas is the most detailed, providing three distinct proofs.
3
Analyze the three aspects of the qisas verse
From Surah al-Baqara 2:178, the author extracts three arguments: (1) preservation of the name 'imān' despite qisas for murder (a major sin), (2) preservation of brotherhood (ukhūwa) between killer and victim's family, (3) the sinner not being deprived of divine leniency and mercy. Understand each point.
These three aspects collectively refute the Kharijite and Mu'tazilite positions.
4
Consider the broader implication
The author concludes with additional verses and notes that the entire Muslim community prays over deceased sinners as believers. Ask yourself: What does this imply about the relationship between sin and faith?
This supports the Sunni view that faith is not nullified by major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على صحة ما ذهب إليه أهل الحق أن الله تعالى أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجود ما عليه الوعيد بقوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَقْرَبُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَأَنْتُمْ سُكَارَى﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا﴾ أبقى لهما اسم الإيمان مع أن إحداهما باغية، وقوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ﴾ وفي الآية دلالة من ثلاثة أوجه:
أحدها: أنه أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجوب القصاص الذي هو حكم قتل العمد الخالي عن الشبه كلها، ولا شك في كونه كبيرة.
والثاني: أنه أبقى اسم الأخوة الثابتة بالإيمان بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا الْمُؤْمِنُونَ إِخْوَةٌ﴾ بين القاتل وأولياء المقتول بقوله: ﴿فَمَنْ عُفِيَ لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ فَاتِّبَاعٌ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ﴾ الآية.
والثالث: أنه تعالى ما أخرج مرتكب هذه الكبيرة عن استحقاق التخفيف والمرحمة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ذَلِكَ تَخْفِيفٌ مِنْ رَبِّكُمْ وَرَحْمَةٌ﴾.
وحكى الاستدلال من هذه الأوجه الثلاثة على نحو ما بينا عن عبد الله بن مسعود – رضي الله عنهما – وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَلَمْ يُهَاجِرُوا﴾ أبقى لمن لم يهاجر اسم الإيمان مع عظم الوعيد في ترك الهجرة بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ تَوَفَّاهُمُ الْمَلَائِكَةُ ظَالِمِي أَنْفُسِهِمْ﴾ مع أنه قال في آخر الآية: ﴿مَا لَكُمْ مِنْ وَلَايَتِهِمْ مِنْ شَيْءٍ﴾ ثم هذا جعلهم مؤمنين وأوجب على المؤمنين نصرهم عند استنصارهم وقوله: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَتَّخِذُوا عَدُوِّي وَعَدُوَّكُمْ أَوْلِيَاءَ﴾ وقوله: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَخُونُوا اللَّهَ وَالرَّسُولَ وَتَخُونُوا أَمَانَاتِكُمْ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَتُوبُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ جَمِيعًا أَيُّهَا الْمُؤْمِنُونَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تُفْلِحُونَ﴾ وفي الباب آيات كثيرة، وكذا الأمة بأسرهم توارثوا الصلاة على...
Then the proof for the correctness of what the People of Truth hold is that Allah, exalted, preserved the name of faith despite the presence of the threat upon them by His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not approach prayer while you are drunk' (4:43), and His saying: 'And if two groups among the believers fight, then make peace between them' (49:9) — He preserved for them the name of faith even though one of them is aggressor. And His saying: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution' (2:178). And in this verse there is an indication from three aspects:

First: He preserved the name of faith despite the obligation of legal retribution, which is the ruling of intentional murder devoid of all ambiguities, and there is no doubt that it is a major sin.

Second: He preserved the name of brotherhood established by faith by His saying: 'The believers are but brothers' (49:10) between the killer and the guardians of the killed by His saying: 'But whoever is pardoned by his brother, then follow-up with goodness' (2:179) — the verse.

Third: He, exalted, did not remove the committer of this major sin from deserving leniency and mercy by His saying: 'That is a lightening from your Lord and mercy' (2:178).

And he reported the argument from these three aspects in the manner we explained from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud — may Allah be pleased with them both. And His saying: 'And those who believed and did not emigrate' (8:72) — He preserved for those who did not emigrate the name of faith despite the severity of the threat in abandoning emigration by His saying: 'Indeed, those whom the angels take in death while they are wronging themselves' (4:97), along with His saying at the end of the verse: 'You have no guardianship over them' (8:72). Then He made them believers and obligated upon the believers to support them when they seek support. And His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not take My enemy and your enemy as allies' (60:1). And His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not betray Allah and the Messenger and betray your trusts' (8:27). And His saying: 'And turn to Allah in repentance, all of you, O believers, that you might succeed' (24:31). And on this topic there are many verses. Similarly, the entire community has inherited the prayer over...
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalil) from the Qur'an supporting the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth, i.e., Ahl al-Sunnah) that a person who commits a major sin (kabira) remains a believer and does not exit the faith. The author cites several verses where Allah addresses sinners as 'believers' (mu'minun). The first set of verses: (1) Surah al-Nisa' 4:43 prohibits approaching prayer while drunk, yet calls the drunk 'O you who have believed.' (2) Surah al-Hujurat 49:9 addresses two fighting groups as 'believers' even though one is aggressor. (3) Surah al-Baqara 2:178 prescribes qisas (retribution) for murder, and the author extracts three distinct arguments from this verse: First, the name of faith (imān) is preserved despite the obligation of qisas for intentional murder, which is undoubtedly a major sin. Second, the brotherhood (ukhūwa) established by faith is maintained between the killer and the victim's family, as the verse refers to the victim's guardian as 'his brother' (min akhīhi). Third, the verse states that qisas is a 'lightening and mercy' from Allah, showing that the sinner is not deprived of Allah's compassion. The author notes that Abdullah ibn Mas'ud used these three points. The author then provides more verses: Surah al-Anfal 8:72 calls those who believed but did not emigrate 'believers' despite the severe threat for not emigrating (Surah al-Nisa' 4:97). Surah al-Mumtahina 60:1 and Surah al-Anfal 8:27 address believers who might commit sins of taking enemies as allies or betraying trusts. Surah al-Nur 24:31 commands repentance to believers. The author concludes that such verses are numerous and that the entire community has inherited the practice of praying over [deceased believers, implying that even sinners are prayed over as believers].

Key Terms
أهل الحق
الإيمان
الكبيرة
القصاص
الأخوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that committing a major sin (like murder) removes one entirely from the fold of faith.
Correction: The Qur'an continues to refer to such individuals as believers, as shown in multiple verses, so iman remains despite major sins.

Checkpoint
What are the three aspects derived from the verse on qisas (2:178) that prove imān remains despite major sins?
A) Preservation of faith, brotherhood, and the absence of punishment.
B) Preservation of faith, brotherhood, and divine mercy/lightening.
C) Preservation of faith, forgiveness, and reward.
D) Preservation of brotherhood, reward, and the threat of hell.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's continued use of 'believers' for sinners is a strong proof that major sins do not invalidate iman.

Faith and Disbelief
beginner

Three Quranic Evidences for the Persistence of Faith Despite Major Sins
Nasafi enumerates three Quranic proofs for faith name remaining.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three direct Quranic proofs that a believer who commits a major sin is still called a believer.

Core Idea
The Quran keeps the name of faith even for those guilty of major sins like murder.

Nasafi presents three clear Quranic evidences that the name 'iman' remains with those who commit major sins. These proofs are essential for refuting the Kharijite and Mu'tazilite positions that major sins negate faith. Let's examine each evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the three evidences
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the three proofs. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice that each evidence begins with a number.
2
Analyze each proof's logic
For each of the three, consider how the verse retains the name of faith despite the sin mentioned. The first is about qisas, the second about brotherhood, the third about alleviation and mercy.
The common theme is that Allah does not remove the title of believer.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Why is it important that the name of faith remains? This refutes groups who say major sins remove faith. Reflect on how this affects our understanding of iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أنه أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجوب القصاص الذي هو حكم قتل العمد الخالي عن الشبه كلها، ولا شك في كونه كبيرة. والثاني: أنه أبقى اسم الأخوة الثابتة بالإيمان بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّمَا الْمُؤْمِنُونَ إِخْوَةٌ ﴾ بين القاتل وأولياء المقتول بقوله: ﴿ فَمَنْ عُفِى لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ فَانْبَاعُ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ ﴾ الآية. والثالث : أنه تعالى ما أخرج مرتكب هذه الكبيرة عن استئهال التخفيف والمرحمة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ ذَلِكَ تَخْفِيفٌ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ وَرَحْمَةٌ ﴾. وحكى الاستدلال من هذه الأوجه الثلاثة على نحو ما بينا عن عبد الله بن مسعود – رضي الله عنهما - وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَلَمْ يُهَاجِرُوا ﴾ أبقى لمن لم يهاجر اسم الإيمان مع عظم الوعيد في ترك الهجرة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ تَوَفَّاهُمُ الْمَلَائِكَةُ ظَالِمِي أَنفُسِهِمْ ﴾ مع أنه قال في آخر الآية: ﴿ مَا لَكُم مِّن وَلَايَتِهِم مِّن شَيْءٍ ﴾ ثم هذا جعلهم مؤمنين وأوجب على المؤمنين نصرهم عند استنصارهم وقوله: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَتَّخِذُوا عَدُوِّي وَعَدُوَّكُمْ أَوْلِيَاءَ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَخُونُوا اللَّهَ وَالرَّسُولَ وَتَخُونُوا أَمَانَاتِكُمْ ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَتُوبُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ جَمِيعًا أَيُّهَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تُفْلِحُونَ ﴾ وفي الباب آيات كثيرة، وكذا الأمة بأسرهم توارثوا الصلاة على من مات من أهل القبلة والاستغفار لهم والترحم عليهم من غير التفحص عن حالة أنه ارتكب كبيرة أو لم يرتكب.
One of them: that He kept the name of faith despite the obligation of retribution which is the ruling of intentional killing free from all doubts, and there is no doubt that it is a major sin. The second: that He kept the name of brotherhood established by faith by His saying: 'Indeed the believers are brothers' between the killer and the heirs of the slain by His saying: 'Then whoever is forgiven something by his brother, let him follow with goodness' the verse. The third: that He, exalted, did not remove the perpetrator of this major sin from deserving alleviation and mercy by His saying: 'That is an alleviation from your Lord and a mercy'. And he narrated the inference from these three aspects in the manner we explained from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud - may Allah be pleased with them both - and His saying: 'And those who believed and did not emigrate' kept for those who did not emigrate the name of faith despite the severity of the threat in abandoning emigration by His saying: 'Indeed those whom the angels take while they are wronging themselves', even though He said at the end of the verse: 'You have no guardianship over them at all', then this made them believers and obligated upon the believers to support them when they seek help, and His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not take My enemy and your enemy as allies', and His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not betray Allah and the Messenger, nor betray your trusts', and His saying: 'And repent to Allah, all of you, O believers, that you may succeed'. And in this chapter there are many verses. Likewise, the entire community has inherited praying for whoever dies among the people of the qibla, and seeking forgiveness for them, and having mercy on them, without scrutinizing whether he committed a major sin or not.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents three Quranic evidences that the name of faith (iman) remains with the believer even when he commits a major sin, and it also notes the consensus of the community in treating such persons as believers. The author, Nasafi, begins by stating the first evidence: Allah retained the name of faith for the intentional murderer despite the obligation of retribution (qisas) which is the ruling for intentional killing free from any doubt, and there is no doubt that such killing is a major sin. The second evidence: Allah kept the name of brotherhood established by faith between the killer and the heirs of the slain, as indicated in the verse on qisas where the killer is called a brother (akh) of the victim's family. The third evidence: Allah did not exclude the perpetrator of this major sin from deserving the alleviation and mercy mentioned in the verse about diya (blood money) being a lightening from the Lord and a mercy. Nasafi then references Abdullah ibn Mas'ud who used these three aspects to argue for the persistence of faith. He then adduces additional verses: the verse about those who believed but did not emigrate – Allah calls them believers despite the severe threat for abandoning hijra, and even says the angels will take them while they are wronging themselves, yet He still obligates believers to help them if they seek help. Other verses address believers directly (e.g., not to take enemies as allies, not to betray trusts, to repent) and address them as believers despite their sins. Nasafi concludes that the entire ummah has consistently prayed funeral prayers for anyone from the people of the qibla, sought forgiveness for them, and shown mercy, without checking whether they had committed a major sin or not. This demonstrates the consensus of the community that major sins do not remove the name of faith.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الكبيرة
القصاص
الأخوة
التخفيف
المرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Major sins expel a person from faith entirely (the view of Khawarij and Mu'tazila).
Correction: The three Quranic evidences show that the name 'iman' remains with the believer despite committing major sins like murder.

Checkpoint
What is the first evidence mentioned for the name of faith remaining despite a major sin?
A) Allah kept brotherhood between killer and heirs.
B) Allah obligated retribution yet kept the name of faith.
C) Allah promised alleviation and mercy.
D) The community prays for all believers.

Key Takeaway
The name of faith is not negated by major sins; the Quran itself attests to this through these three proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin—even a major one—do you truly believe that you are still a believer? Or does a voice inside you whisper that you have lost your faith? Based on this lesson, will you actively remind yourself of Allah's preservation of your iman?
?
Think of a Muslim you know who has fallen into a grave sin. How do you view them? Are they still your brother or sister in faith? This lesson says yes. Will you change how you treat them, offering mercy instead of condemnation?
?
If you were the one who committed murder (may Allah protect you), would you still hope for Allah's mercy? This lesson shows that even in that extreme case, mercy is available. How does that knowledge affect your fear of Allah and your desire to repent?

Faith and Disbelief

Additional Quranic Evidence and Community Consensus on Faith Remaining
Nasafi cites additional verses and community consensus to support persistence of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how additional Quranic verses and the consistent practice of the Muslim community affirm that major sins do not remove the name of faith.

Core Idea
The Quran addresses sinners as believers and the community treats them as believers.

After presenting three direct evidences, Nasafi cites further Quranic verses that call sinners believers, and notes the continuous practice of the community to treat all who pray towards the qibla as believers. This lesson explores these additional proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the additional evidence from the non-emigration verse
Read the verse about those who believed but did not emigrate. Notice that Allah calls them believers despite the severe threat for abandoning hijra.
The verse shows that even those who commit the major sin of not emigrating are still called believers.
2
Examine other Quranic addresses to believers
Read the other verses that address believers directly (e.g., 'O you who have believed...') and command them to avoid sins. These addresses assume the audience is still believers despite their potential sins.
The verbs of prohibition imply that the addressees are capable of sinning, yet they remain believers.
3
Understand the community's practice
Nasafi notes that the entire ummah has consistently prayed funeral prayers for all who died facing the qibla, sought forgiveness for them, and had mercy on them, without investigating whether they committed major sins.
This consensus demonstrates that the community treats all apparent Muslims as believers.

Correction: Nasafi shows that the community prays for all who face the qibla, regardless of their sins, indicating that major sins do not remove the name of faith.

Checkpoint
Which verse is cited to show that Allah keeps the name of faith despite a severe threat for not emigrating?
A) 'Indeed the believers are brothers'
B) 'And those who believed and did not emigrate'
C) 'That is an alleviation from your Lord and a mercy'
D) 'O you who have believed, do not betray Allah'

Key Takeaway
The Quran and the practice of the Muslim community consistently affirm that major sins do not remove the name of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a Muslim committing a major sin, do you still consider them a believer and treat them as such, or do you distance yourself? What would it take for you to pray for them and seek forgiveness for them as the early community did?
?
Have you ever felt that your own sins make you unworthy of being called a believer? How does the Quran's addressing of sinners as 'O believers' challenge that feeling? What does that mean for your hope in Allah's mercy?
?
If you were to die tonight with your current sins, would you expect the Muslim community to pray over you and consider you a believer? If not, what is preventing you from feeling confident in your faith despite your shortcomings?
