Quranic Proof Against Exception in Declaring Iman
Provides Quranic evidence that believers affirm Iman without exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran itself commands believers to declare Iman without making exceptions, and why this refutes those who insist on adding 'insha'Allah' to their profession of faith.

Core Idea
The Quran commands 'say: we believe' without exception—our Iman is affirmed now, not contingent on an unknown future.

This lesson examines the Quranic evidence for the position that believers should declare their Iman definitively, without adding 'if Allah wills' or any exception. The author cites several verses where Allah praises decisive affirmation of Iman and commands it. Understanding these proofs clarifies why the practice of istithna (making exception) in declaring Iman is not required by the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
First, read the Arabic text to get a sense of the author's argument. Notice the Quranic verses quoted.
2
Identify the Quranic verses
Find each Quranic citation in the passage. They are: 'آمَنَ الرَّسُولُ بِمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْهِ...', 'رَبَّنَا إِنَّنَا سَمِعْنَا...', and 'قُولُوا آمَنَّا'. Understand their context.
The verses are from Surah Al-Baqarah, Surah Al-Imran, and Surah Al-Baqarah verse 136.
3
Understand the author's reasoning
The author uses these verses to show that Allah testified to Iman for believers, praised those who said it decisively, and commanded to say it without exception. Then he notes that no one questions permissibility granted by Iman, so why question naming oneself a believer?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that just as we act on things made permissible by Iman without hesitation, we should not hesitate to call ourselves believers without exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الله تعالى شهد بالإيمان لمن آمن بالله ورسله بقوله تعالى: آمَنَ الرَّسُولُ بِمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْهِ مِن رَّبِّهِ وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ ومدح بقطع القول الذين قالوا: رَبَّنَا إِنَّنَا سَمِعْنَا مُنَادِيًا يُنَادِي لِلْإِيمَانِ أَنْ آمِنُوا بِرَبِّكُمْ فَآمَنَّا الآية ولم يأمر بالاستثناء وإن لم يكن لهم بالعاقبة علم، وأمر أيضا بالقول بذلك من غير الاستثناء بقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُولُوا آمَنَّا ﴾ ثم خاطب الله تعالى خلقه في كثير من العبادات باسم الإيمان وفي كثير من الحل والحرمة ثم لم أحد تحرج في شيء مما أحل باسم الإيمان وأمر به ظنًا منه بنفسه أنه ليس يوجد بحقيق لذلك الاسم وأن المراد ينصرف إلى غيره فكذا التسمي به، والله الموفق.
And that which indicates the correctness of what we have held is that Allah Almighty testified to Iman for those who believe in Allah and His Messengers by His saying: 'The Messenger has believed in what was revealed to him from his Lord, and the believers.' And He praised those who said decisively: 'Our Lord, indeed we have heard a caller calling to Iman, [saying], "Believe in your Lord," and we have believed.' the verse, and He did not command exception, even though they had no knowledge of the end. And He also commanded saying that without exception by His saying: 'Say, "We believe."' Then Allah Almighty addressed His creation in many acts of worship by the name of Iman and in many matters of permissibility and prohibition. Then no one felt hesitation in anything that was made permissible by the name of Iman and acted upon it, thinking that it does not exist as a reality for that name and that the intended meaning applies to something else. So likewise is being named with it. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage provides the positive proof for the author's position that no exception should be made when declaring one's Iman. The author cites several Quranic evidences. First, Allah testified to Iman for the Prophet and the believers in Surah Al-Baqarah 285, where they said 'Amanna' without exception. Second, Allah praised those who said 'Rabban... amanna' in Surah Al-Imran 193, those who responded to the caller to Iman and said 'amanna' decisively, without conditional phrasing, even though they did not know their ultimate fate. Third, Allah commanded the believers to say 'Qulu amanna' (Say, 'We believe') without exception. Then the author notes that in many commands regarding worship and lawful/unlawful matters, Allah addressed people using the name 'Iman', and no one felt hesitation in applying that name to themselves thinking that they do not meet the reality of that name or that the intended meaning applies to someone else. Therefore, by analogy, one should not hesitate to call oneself a believer without exception. The passage concludes with 'And Allah is the grantor of success.'

Misconception: Believers must always say 'if Allah wills' when declaring Iman because only Allah knows the end.
Correction: The Quran itself commands saying 'we believe' without exception, even though we do not know our ultimate fate, as seen in the verses quoted.

Key Takeaway
The Quran commands believers to affirm their Iman without making exceptions, and this is the position that avoids introducing doubt into one's faith.

The Identity of Islam and Iman
Declares that Islam and Iman are synonymous and every believer is a Muslim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Maturidi position equates Islam and Iman as synonymous, based on the definition of Iman as tasdiq (affirmation).

Core Idea
Iman and Islam are the same reality under two synonymous names.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi tackles a key question in theology: is there a difference between having faith (iman) and submitting to God (islam)? The author declares firmly that they are one and the same, grounding his argument in the definition of iman as heartfelt affirmation (tasdiq). Let's see how he builds this case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice how the author presents his argument concisely.
The passage ends abruptly; the intended conclusion is clear from the context.
2
Identify the main claim and reasoning
Find the statement that Islam and Iman do not differ, and the reason given: both are defined as tasdiq.
The author uses the phrase 'لا يتغايران' to emphasize non-difference.
3
Understand the role of tasdiq
Tasdiq (affirmation of the heart) is the core definition of Iman in Maturidi theology. The author uses this to equate Islam with Iman.
4
Note the synonymy and mutual inclusion
The author says the names are synonymous and that every believer is a Muslim and every Muslim is a believer.
This reinforces the identity.
5
Consider the common misunderstanding
Some differentiate between Islam as outward submission and Iman as inner faith. The author rejects this distinction.
This lesson guards against that misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسلام والإيمان لا يتغايران وإذا عرف أن الإيمان هو التصديق عرفنا أن الإيمان والإسلام شيء واحد، والأسماء من قبيل الأسماء المترادفة، وكل مؤمن مسلم وكل مسلم مؤمن، وذلك لأن
Islam and Iman do not differ. And when it is known that Iman is tasdiq, we know that Iman and Islam are one thing, and the names are of the category of synonymous names. And every believer is a Muslim and every Muslim is a believer. That is because
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage asserts the identity of Islam and Iman. The author states that Islam and Iman do not differ (لا يتغايران). He gives the reason: since Iman is defined as tasdiq (affirmation of the heart), and Islam is also defined as tasdiq, they are one and the same. The terms are synonymous (أسماء مترادفة). Consequently, every mu'min (believer) is also a muslim (one who submits), and every muslim is a mu'min. The passage seems to cut off, but the point is clear: the author maintains that there is no distinction between the two concepts in terms of their essential reality.

Key Terms
إيمان
إسلام
تصديق
مترادفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Islam is only outward actions and Iman is inner belief, thus a person can be Muslim without being a true believer.
Correction: The author asserts that Iman and Islam are identical, so true Islam requires the inner affirmation that is Iman.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between Iman and Islam?
A) Islam is a subset of Iman.
B) They are synonymous.
C) Iman is a subset of Islam.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Iman and Islam are not two distinct things but two names for the same inner affirmation of the heart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Iman and Islam are truly one reality, where do you notice a gap between your outward practice and your inner conviction? What specific area of your life is not yet unified under this truth?
?
The author defines Iman as tasdiq—affirmation of the heart. What truth about Allah (like His oneness, His names, or His promise) do you struggle to fully affirm deep down? Why, and what one step can you take today to turn that struggle into sincere tasdiq?
?
Consider that every Muslim is a believer in the author's view. Does your treatment of fellow Muslims reflect this? When you judge another Muslim's faith, are you assuming a distinction between Islam and Iman that the author says does not exist? How would your interactions change if you fully believed this identity?
